<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_level_change">Sea level change</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">Sea level</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">Sea level rise</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_(NOAA_GFDL).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="485" data-file-width="650" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_%28NOAA_GFDL%29.png/220px-Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_%28NOAA_GFDL%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_%28NOAA_GFDL%29.png/330px-Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_%28NOAA_GFDL%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_%28NOAA_GFDL%29.png/440px-Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_%28NOAA_GFDL%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_(NOAA_GFDL).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The estimated change in sea level caused by carbon dioxide emissions.</div></div></div>
<p>Global sea level change for much of the last century has generally been estimated using <a href="/wiki/Tide_gauge" title="Tide gauge">tide gauge</a> measurements collated over long periods of time to give a long-term average. More recently, <a href="/wiki/Altimeter" title="Altimeter">altimeter</a> measurements—in combination with accurately determined <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellite</a> orbits—have provided an improved measurement of global sea level change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> To measure sea levels prior to instrumental measurements, scientists have dated <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> that grow near the surface of the ocean, coastal sediments, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_terrace" title="Marine terrace">marine terraces</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ooids" title="Ooids">ooids</a> in <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestones</a>, and nearshore archaeological remains. The predominant dating methods used are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium-thorium_dating" title="Uranium-thorium dating">uranium series</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radiocarbon</a>, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmogenic_radionuclide_dating" title="Cosmogenic radionuclide dating">cosmogenic radionuclides</a> being sometimes used to date terraces that have experienced relative sea level fall. In the early <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a>, global temperatures were 1–2˚C warmer than the present temperature, yet sea level was 15–25 meters higher than today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to recent studies, global-mean sea level rose by 195 mm during the period from 1870 to 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Since 2004, satellite-based records indicate that there has been a further 43 mm of global-mean sea levels rise, as of  July 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Climate_change&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ice_cores">Ice cores</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="372" data-file-width="1024" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg/220px-Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg/330px-Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg/440px-Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Antarctic temperature changes during the last several <a href="/wiki/Glacial_period" title="Glacial period">glacial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">interglacial</a> cycles of the present <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a>, according to <a href="/wiki/%CE%9418O" title="Δ18O">δ18O</a> ratios.</div></div></div>
<p>Analysis of ice in a core drilled from an <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">ice sheet</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a>, can be used to show a link between temperature and global sea level variations. The air trapped in bubbles in the ice can also reveal the CO<sub>2</sub> variations of the atmosphere from the distant past, well before modern environmental influences. The study of these ice cores has been a significant indicator of the changes in CO<sub>2</sub> over many millennia, and continues to provide valuable information about the differences between ancient and modern atmospheric conditions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cloud_cover_and_precipitation">Cloud cover and precipitation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">Cloud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">Precipitation</a></div>
<p>Past precipitation can be estimated in the modern era with the global network of precipitation gauges. Surface coverage over oceans and remote areas is relatively sparse, but, reducing reliance on <a href="/wiki/Interpolation" title="Interpolation">interpolation</a>, satellite clouds and precipitation data has been available since the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IJC_precip_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-IJC_precip-92">[92]</a></sup> Quantification of climatological variation of precipitation in prior centuries and epochs is less complete but approximated using proxies such as marine sediments, ice cores, cave stalagmites, and tree rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> In July 2016 scientists published evidence of increased cloud cover over polar regions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> as predicted by climate models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Climatological temperatures substantially affect cloud cover and precipitation. For instance, during the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a> of 18,000 years ago, thermal-driven <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> from the oceans onto continental landmasses was low, causing large areas of extreme desert, including <a href="/wiki/Polar_desert" title="Polar desert">polar deserts</a> (cold but with low rates of cloud cover and precipitation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ORNL_paleoclimate_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-ORNL_paleoclimate-96">[96]</a></sup> In contrast, the world's climate was cloudier and wetter than today near the start of the warm <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_period" title="Atlantic period">Atlantic Period</a> of 8000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ORNL_paleoclimate_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-ORNL_paleoclimate-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimated global land precipitation increased by approximately 2% over the course of the 20th century, though the calculated trend varies if different time endpoints are chosen, complicated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o-Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño-Southern Oscillation">ENSO</a> and other oscillations, including greater global land cloud cover precipitation in the 1950s and 1970s than the later 1980s and 1990s despite the positive trend over the century overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IJC_precip_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-IJC_precip-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_watercycletrend_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS_watercycletrend-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
Similar slight overall increase in global river runoff and in average soil moisture has been perceived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_watercycletrend_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS_watercycletrend-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vegetation">Vegetation</span></h3>
<p>A change in the type, distribution and coverage of vegetation may occur given a change in the climate. Some changes in climate may result in increased precipitation and warmth, resulting in improved plant growth and the subsequent sequestration of airborne CO<sub>2</sub>. A gradual increase in warmth in a region will lead to earlier flowering and fruiting times, driving a change in the timing of life cycles of dependent organisms. Conversely, cold will cause plant bio-cycles to lag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Larger, faster or more radical changes, however, may result in vegetation stress, rapid plant loss and <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a> in certain circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> An example of this occurred during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carboniferous_Rainforest_Collapse" title="Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse">Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse</a> (CRC), an extinction event 300 million years ago. At this time vast rainforests covered the equatorial region of Europe and America. Climate change devastated these tropical rainforests, abruptly fragmenting the habitat into isolated 'islands' and causing the extinction of many plant and animal species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse-100">[100]</a></sup>   Such stress can alter the growth rate of trees, which allows scientists to infer climate trends by analyzing the growth rate of tree rings.  This branch of climate science is called <a href="/wiki/Dendroclimatology" title="Dendroclimatology">dendroclimatology</a>, and is one of the many ways they research <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">climate trends prior to written records</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Forest_genetic_resources">Forest genetic resources</span></h4>
<p>Even though this is a field with many uncertainties, it is expected that over the next 50 years climate changes will have an effect on the diversity of <a href="/wiki/Forest_genetic_resources" title="Forest genetic resources">forest genetic resources</a> and thereby on the distribution of forest tree species and the composition of forests. Diversity of <a href="/wiki/Forest_genetic_resources" title="Forest genetic resources">forest genetic resources</a> enables the potential for a species (or a population) to adapt to climatic changes and related future challenges such as temperature changes, drought, pests, diseases and forest fire. However, species are not naturally capable to adapt in the pace of which the climate is changing and the increasing temperatures will most likely facilitate the spread of pests and diseases, creating an additional threat to forest trees and their populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> To inhibit these problems human interventions, such as transfer of <a href="/wiki/Forest_reproductive_material" title="Forest reproductive material">forest reproductive material</a>, may be needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollen_analysis">Pollen analysis</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Palynology" title="Palynology">Palynology</a> is the study of contemporary and fossil <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palynomorph" title="Palynomorph">palynomorphs</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>. Palynology is used to infer the geographical distribution of plant species, which vary under different climate conditions. Different groups of plants have pollen with distinctive shapes and surface textures, and since the outer surface of pollen is composed of a very resilient material, they resist decay. Changes in the type of pollen found in different layers of sediment in lakes, bogs, or river deltas indicate changes in plant communities. These changes are often a sign of a changing climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> As an example, palynological studies have been used to track changing vegetation patterns throughout the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">Quaternary glaciations</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> and especially since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_maximum" title="Last glacial maximum">last glacial maximum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="994" data-file-width="558" height="392" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg/220px-Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg/330px-Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg/440px-Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Top:</i> <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">Arid</a> ice age climate<div style="clear:both;"></div><i>Middle:</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_period" title="Atlantic period">Atlantic Period</a>, warm and wet<div style="clear:both;"></div><i>Bottom:</i> Potential vegetation in climate now if not for human effects like agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ORNL_paleoclimate_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-ORNL_paleoclimate-96">[96]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span></h3>
<p>Remains of <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a> are common in freshwater and land sediments. Different species of beetles tend to be found under different climatic conditions. Given the extensive lineage of beetles whose genetic makeup has not altered significantly over the millennia, knowledge of the present climatic range of the different species, and the age of the sediments in which remains are found, past climatic conditions may be inferred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coope1999_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coope1999-109">[109]</a></sup>
The studies of the impact in vertebrates are few mainly from <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>, where there are the fewest studies; between 1970 and 2012, vertebrates declined by 58 percent, with freshwater, marine, and terrestrial populations declining by 81, 36, and 35 percent, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, the historical abundance of various <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> species has been found to have a substantial relationship with observed climatic conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Changes in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_productivity" title="Primary productivity">primary productivity</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autotrophs" title="Autotrophs">autotrophs</a> in the oceans can affect marine food webs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_and_archaeological_evidence">Historical and archaeological evidence</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Historical_impacts_of_climate_change" title="Historical impacts of climate change">Historical impacts of climate change</a></div>
<p>Climate change in the recent past may be detected by corresponding changes in settlement and agricultural patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demenocal01_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demenocal01-113">[113]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological" title="Archaeological">Archaeological</a> evidence, <a href="/wiki/Oral_history" title="Oral history">oral history</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_documents" title="Historical documents">historical documents</a> can offer insights into past changes in the climate. Climate change effects have been linked to the rise<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-viking01_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-viking01-114">[114]</a></sup> and also the collapse of various civilizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demenocal01_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Demenocal01-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glacier_Mass_Balance.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="658" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Glacier_Mass_Balance.png/250px-Glacier_Mass_Balance.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Glacier_Mass_Balance.png/375px-Glacier_Mass_Balance.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Glacier_Mass_Balance.png/500px-Glacier_Mass_Balance.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glacier_Mass_Balance.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Decline in thickness of glaciers worldwide over the past half-century</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_climate_change_science" title="History of climate change science">History of climate change science</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Historical_climatology" title="Historical climatology">Historical climatology</a> is the study of historical changes in climate and their effect on human history and development. The primary sources include written records such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sagas" title="Sagas">sagas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chronicle" title="Chronicle">chronicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Map" title="Map">maps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Local_history" title="Local history">local history</a> literature as well as pictorial representations such as <a href="/wiki/Painting" title="Painting">paintings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drawing" title="Drawing">drawings</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Rock_art" title="Rock art">rock art</a>. This differs from <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">paleoclimatology</a> which encompasses climate change over the entire <a href="/wiki/History_of_Earth" title="History of Earth">history of Earth</a>. Notable climate events known to paleoclimatology are provided in this <a href="/wiki/List_of_periods_and_events_in_climate_history" title="List of periods and events in climate history">list of periods and events in climate history</a>.
</p><p>Prior to the 18th century, scientists had not suspected that prehistoric climates were different from the modern period. By the late 18th century, geologists found evidence of a succession of <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">geological ages</a> with changes in climate. There were various competing theories about these changes, and <a href="/wiki/James_Hutton" title="James Hutton">James Hutton</a>, whose ideas of cyclic change over huge periods of time were later dubbed <a href="/wiki/Uniformitarianism" title="Uniformitarianism">uniformitarianism</a>, was among those who found signs of past glacial activity in places too warm for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciers" title="Glaciers">glaciers</a> in modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-115">[115]</a></sup> In 1815 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Perraudin" title="Jean-Pierre Perraudin">Jean-Pierre Perraudin</a> described for the first time how glaciers might be responsible for the giant boulders seen in alpine valleys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-paleo_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-paleo-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of the 19th century, scientific opinion had turned decisively against any belief in a human influence on climate. And whatever the regional effects, few imagined that humans could affect the climate of the planet as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aiphist2_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-aiphist2-117">[117]</a></sup> However, in 1899 <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Chrowder_Chamberlin" title="Thomas Chrowder Chamberlin">Thomas Chrowder Chamberlin</a> developed at length the idea that changes in climate could result from changes in the concentration of atmospheric carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH1965_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-WH1965-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1967, taking advantage of the ability of digital computers to integrate absorption curves numerically, <a href="/wiki/Syukuro_Manabe" title="Syukuro Manabe">Syukuro Manabe</a> and Richard Wetherald made the first detailed calculation of the greenhouse effect incorporating convection (the "Manabe-Wetherald one-dimensional radiative-convective model").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> They found that, in the absence of unknown feedbacks such as changes in clouds, a doubling of carbon dioxide from the current level would result in approximately 2 °C increase in global temperature.
</p><p>In 1985 a joint UNEP/WMO/ICSU Conference on the "Assessment of the Role of Carbon Dioxide and Other Greenhouse Gases in Climate Variations and Associated Impacts" concluded that greenhouse gases "are expected" to cause significant warming in the next century and that some warming is inevitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Since the 1990s, scientific research on climate change has included multiple disciplines and has expanded. Research during this period has been summarized in the Assessment Reports by the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a>.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<cite class="citation book">America's Climate Choices: Panel on Advancing the Science of Climate Change; National Research Council (2010). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140529161102/http://www.nap.edu/catalog.php?record_id=12782" rel="nofollow"><i>Advancing the Science of Climate Change</i></a>. Washington, D.C.: The National Academies Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-309-14588-0" title="Special:BookSources/0-309-14588-0">0-309-14588-0</a>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.nap.edu/catalog.php?record_id=12782" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 29 May 2014. <q>(p1) ... there is a strong, credible body of evidence, based on multiple lines of research, documenting that climate is changing and that these changes are in large part caused by human activities. While much remains to be learned, the core phenomenon, scientific questions, and hypotheses have been examined thoroughly and have stood firm in the face of serious scientific debate and careful evaluation of alternative explanations. (pp. 21–22) Some scientific conclusions or theories have been so thoroughly examined and tested, and supported by so many independent observations and results, that their likelihood of subsequently being found to be wrong is vanishingly small. Such conclusions and theories are then regarded as settled facts. This is the case for the conclusions that the Earth system is warming and that much of this warming is very likely due to human activities.</q></cite><span class="Z3988"
